\[

We need to find the exact set of őĽ for which Alice can force a win, and for which Bazza can force a win. The game is symmetric in a sense but with different constraints. This is reminiscent of a "game of inequalities" often solved by considering "potential functions" or "invariants". We might try to find a "critical" őĽ such that if őĽ > some threshold, Alice can force a win; if őĽ < some threshold, Bazza can force a win; and at the threshold, it's a draw. But we need to determine these thresholds precisely.

From the analysis, we have:
- For őĽ = 1, Alice can win by playing x_1=0, x_3=‚ąö5.
- For őĽ = 0.5, Bazza can win by playing x_2=‚ąö2, x_4=‚ąö2, etc.
- For őĽ = 0.8, we saw that if Bazza plays x_2=1, Alice might not win immediately, but perhaps she can still win later? We need to analyze the game in general.

Let's try to find a more systematic approach.

We have an infinite game. Define the state after each turn as (S, Q). The constraints:
- After an odd turn n, S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n, and Q_n is whatever.
- After an even turn n, Q_n ‚Č§ n, and S_n is whatever.

The players alternate: Alice on odd, Bazza on even. They can choose any nonnegative x_n.

We want to know who can force a win.

Observation: The game is "monotonic" in the sense that S_n and Q_n are nondecreasing. Also, the constraints are cumulative.

We can try to see if there is a "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy. Since both players can always choose 0, the game can be drawn if both players avoid making a move that leads to the opponent's loss. But the players want to win, so they will try to force a win if possible.

Typically, in such games, the winning player can force a win by "copying" the opponent's moves or by using a "potential" that shows that if the opponent plays a certain way, the player can respond to cause a violation.

We can try to find an invariant that gives a necessary condition for the game to continue indefinitely. For instance, we have S_n^2 ‚Č§ n Q_n. So if at the start of Alice's turn on odd n, we have S_{n-1} > őĽ n, she loses. But if the game goes on forever, then for all odd n, S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ n, and for all even n, Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n (since the constraint for even n is Q_n ‚Č§ n, so at start of even turn, Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n? Actually, for even n, the constraint is Q_n ‚Č§ n. So before his move, Q_{n-1} is the sum of squares after Alice's previous odd move. There is no explicit constraint on Q_{n-1} other than that it must be ‚Č§ something? Not necessarily; it could be > n, but then he would be unable to move. So for the game to continue, we must have Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n for all even n, because if Q_{n-1} > n, then Bazza would lose immediately. Similarly, for all odd n, S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ n. So the game can continue only if these inequalities hold for all n.

So if Alice wants to win, she wants to eventually make Q_{n-1} > n for some even n. She can try to build up Q. Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > őĽ n for some odd n.

Thus, we need to analyze the maximum possible Q given the linear budget, and vice versa.

We can think of the game as a "two-player" control problem. Since both players can choose 0, they can always avoid causing the opponent's loss. So if a player has a winning strategy, it must involve making a move that forces the opponent to be unable to move, and the opponent cannot avoid that because any move they make leads to a losing position. So we need to find if there is a "critical" value őĽ0 such that if őĽ > őĽ0, Alice can force a win, and if őĽ < őĽ0, Bazza can force a win. And at őĽ = őĽ0, perhaps both can avoid losing (draw). We already have some evidence: őĽ=0.5, Bazza wins; őĽ=1, Alice wins. So the threshold is somewhere between 0.5 and 1. But we need to find the exact threshold.

Let's try to analyze the game more systematically. We can attempt to find a "potential" function that is monotonic and bounded, and show that if őĽ is too large, Alice can force it to exceed some bound, and if too small, Bazza can force it.

Consider the quantity A_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. We have S_n^2 ‚Č§ n Q_n, so A_n ‚Č§ 0. Also, we have A_n = (S_{n-1}+x_n)^2 - n (Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) = S_{n-1}^2 + 2S_{n-1}x_n + x_n^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x_n^2 = (S_{n-1}^2 - n Q_{n-1}) + 2S_{n-1}x_n + (1-n)x_n^2. But note that n Q_{n-1} is not directly related to the previous constraint. Actually, the previous constraint for the turn is: if n is odd, S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ (n-1) (since S_{n-1} was the sum after the previous turn, and that bound is őĽ (n-1) because n-1 is odd? Wait, careful: The constraints are applied at the turn when the player moves. So after turn n-1, we have S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ (n-1) if n-1 is odd, and Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n-1 if n-1 is even. So we can use these.

We can try to analyze the game as a "minimax" where each player tries to maximize or minimize some function. Perhaps we can find a "strategy" for one player to keep the game going forever, and a "strategy" for the other to force a win when őĽ is above/below a certain threshold.

Another approach: Since both players can choose 0, the game is "draw" if both players play 0. But the problem likely expects that one of them has a winning strategy for all őĽ except maybe a specific value. But we need to determine the sets.

We need to analyze the game as a "control" problem.

Let's think of it as a "game of inequalities" where the players can "spend" their budget. The total "budget" for each player is not fixed but grows linearly. Actually, the constraints are that after each turn, the sum of the chosen numbers (linear) is at most őĽ n for odd n, and the sum of squares is at most n for even n. So we can think of the players as "sharing" the resource of the linear sum and the square sum. The game ends when either the linear sum exceeds the linear bound for an odd turn, or the square sum exceeds the square bound for an even turn.

Because the game is infinite, the players can play "safe" moves that keep both sums within bounds. The question is: Can one player force a violation of the opponent's bound? This is reminiscent of a "game of reaching a target" with two resources.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Alice to win for any őĽ > 1? But we already have őĽ=1.1, she can win quickly. For őĽ=0.9, we need to see if she can still win. The above attempt with x_1=0.9 and x_2=1.0 (max S_2) didn't give immediate win, but she might still win later. Let's simulate more carefully for őĽ=0.9.

We need to find if there exists a strategy for Alice that guarantees that at some even turn, Q_n > n+1. She can choose her x_n on odd turns. Bazza will try to avoid that. So we can think of this as a "pursuit" where Bazza tries to keep the sum of squares low, and also he tries to make the linear sum high to cause Alice's loss. But if Alice's strategy is to play 0 on all odd turns, then she never wins. So she must play positive numbers at some point. The key is to see if she can force a win even if Bazza plays optimally to avoid it.

So it appears that for őĽ=0.75, the pattern stabilizes with x_odd = 0.088 and x_even = 1.412. So the sum S_{2k} = (2k-1)őĽ + (k-1)*1.412? Actually, the sum of the first k even terms is about 1.4142 + 1.140 + 1.368 + ... + 1.412, but the early ones are different. However, the difference between S_{2k} and (2k+1)őĽ is the őī_k? Actually, we have őī_k = (2k+1)őĽ - S_{2k}. For large k, we have S_{2k} = (2k-1)őĽ + ‚ąö(2 - őī_{k-1}^2). Since őī_{k-1} is constant, say őī, then S_{2k} = (2k-1)őĽ + ‚ąö(2 - őī^2). So őī_k = (2k+1)őĽ - S_{2k} = (2k+1)őĽ - [(2k-1)őĽ + ‚ąö(2-őī^2)] = 2őĽ - ‚ąö(2-őī^2). So őī_k is constant. In our simulation, őī_k is about 0.088. So indeed, for őĽ=0.75, the recurrence gives őī_k = 0.088. So Alice can move with x_{2k+1}=0.088. So it's a draw.

So the violation condition for a player to be unable to move is that the cumulative sum (or sum of squares) before the move is already too high to allow any nonnegative choice (including 0) to satisfy the constraint for the turn. That is, if before Alice's turn on odd n, we have S_{n-1} > őĽ n, then even if she chooses 0, S_{n-1}+0 > őĽ n, so she cannot move. But note: The constraint for odd n is S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n. So if S_{n-1} > őĽ n, she cannot choose any x_n ‚Č• 0 to keep S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n because S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n ‚Č• S_{n-1} > őĽ n. So she loses if at the start of her turn, S_{n-1} > őĽ n. Similarly, before Bazza's turn on even n, if Q_{n-1} > n, then even with x_n=0, Q_n = Q_{n-1} > n, so he cannot move. So the game ends when a player's cumulative sum or sum of squares exceeds the bound for the next turn.

Thus, the players try to keep the cumulative sums within bounds so that the opponent cannot force a violation. But they also want to force the opponent into a situation where the opponent's cumulative sum is already too high. So it's a kind of "push" game.

We need to determine for which őĽ Alice can force a win. Since she moves on odd turns, she might try to increase the sum of squares enough to make Bazza's constraint impossible at an even turn. Alternatively, Bazza might try to increase the sum (linear) to make Alice's constraint impossible at an odd turn.

Because the constraints involve n, we can think of average behavior. Alice's constraint is about average of x_i: (sum x_i)/n ‚Č§ őĽ. So Alice tries to keep the average ‚Č§ őĽ. Bazza's constraint is about average of squares: (sum x_i^2)/n ‚Č§ 1. So Bazza tries to keep the root-mean-square ‚Č§ 1. So the players are essentially controlling two different statistics.

Notice that if őĽ is large, Alice has a lot of room, she can choose large numbers, but that might make the sum of squares large, which could hurt Bazza. Conversely, if őĽ is small, Alice's constraint is tight, she cannot choose large numbers, but Bazza's constraint is about sum of squares; he can choose numbers to keep sum of squares low, but Alice might try to force a violation by making sum of squares large.

We need to analyze the game as a kind of "minimax" or "control" game. Since both can always choose 0, the game can be drawn if both avoid making a move that leads to opponent's loss. But the players want to win, so they will try to force a win if possible.

We need to find if there is a threshold where one player can force a win. Usually in such games, the threshold is őĽ = sqrt(2)? Or something like that. Let's try to reason.

Define the state after each turn. Let‚Äôs denote after turn n, we have S_n and Q_n. The constraints: For odd n, S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n; for even n, Q_n ‚Č§ n.

We want to see if Alice can force Bazza to be unable to move at some even turn. That would require that at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n, but note that after Alice's move on turn n-1 (odd), we have Q_{n-1} = Q_{n-2} + x_{n-1}^2. So she can try to make Q_{n-1} large. However, she also must respect her own constraint S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ (n-1). So she cannot arbitrarily increase x_{n-1} because that would increase S. But she might choose x_{n-1} large, as long as S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ (n-1). Since S_{n-2} might already be close to the bound, she might have limited room.

Similarly, Bazza on even n can try to make S_n large to make Alice's next constraint impossible. He chooses x_n such that Q_n ‚Č§ n, but he can also increase S_n by choosing x_n > 0, as long as Q_n ‚Č§ n. So he can try to push the linear sum up.

Thus, we need to find the "critical" őĽ where the players can maintain a "safe" region indefinitely, or one can force a win.

We can consider the game as a kind of "alternating constraints" with a "budget". Perhaps we can find an invariant or a strategy for one player to keep the game going forever, and a strategy for the other to force a win when őĽ is above/below some threshold.

Let's try to think of a strategy for Bazza to win for small őĽ. Suppose őĽ is very small, e.g., őĽ = 0.1. Then Alice's constraint is very tight: the sum of x_i must be ‚Č§ 0.1 n. Given őĽ, we need to see if Alice can force a win. Similarly, Bazza can try to force Alice to lose.

Because both players can choose 0, the game can always be drawn if both players are content to play 0. But in a competitive setting, they want to win, so they will try to force a win if possible. The question: For which őĽ does Alice have a winning strategy? That means there exists a strategy for Alice that guarantees that eventually Bazza cannot move, no matter how Bazza plays. Similarly for Bazza.

We need to analyze the game as a "positional" game with infinite horizon. Possibly we can find a "critical" value őĽ0 such that if őĽ > őĽ0, Alice can force a win; if őĽ < őĽ0, Bazza can force a win; if őĽ = őĽ0, the game is a draw (both can avoid losing, and no forced win). But we need to determine these sets.

Let's try to formalize the game state.

After each turn, we have two numbers: S_n and Q_n. But the constraints are only applied at the turn. For an odd turn n (Alice), the condition is S_{n-1} + x_n ‚Č§ őĽ n. We can think of the game as a "pursuit" where each player tries to increase the opposite statistic to force a violation. We can think of the game as a "resource" allocation: each turn, a player decides how much to allocate to the cumulative sum (S) and sum of squares (Q), but there are constraints on the totals.

From the analysis, we have:
- For őĽ = 1, Alice can win by playing x_1=0, x_3=‚ąö5.

Let's try to find a more systematic approach.

We have an infinite game. Define the state after each turn as (S, Q). The constraints:
- After an odd turn n, S_n ‚Č§ őĽ n, and Q_n is whatever.
- After an even turn n, Q_n ‚Č§ n, and S_n is whatever.

The players alternate: Alice on odd, Bazza on even. They can choose any nonnegative x_n.

We want to know who can force a win.

Observation: The game is "monotonic" in the sense that S_n and Q_n are nondecreasing. Also, the constraints are cumulative.

We can try to see if there is a "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy. Since both players can always choose 0, the game can be drawn if both players avoid making a move that leads to the opponent's loss. But the players want to win, so they will try to force a win if possible.

Typically, in such games, the winning player can force a win by "copying" the opponent's moves or by using a "potential" that shows that if the opponent plays a certain way, the player can respond to cause a violation.

We can try to find an invariant that gives a necessary condition for the game to continue indefinitely. For instance, we have S_n^2 ‚Č§ n Q_n. So if at the start of Alice's turn on odd n, we have S_{n-1} > őĽ n, she loses. But if the game goes on forever, then for all odd n, S_{n-1} ‚Č§ őĽ n, and for all even n, Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n (since the constraint for even n is Q_n ‚Č§ n, so at start of even turn, Q_{n-1} ‚Č§ n? So the game can continue only if these inequalities hold for all n.

So if Alice wants to win, she wants to eventually make Q_{n-1} > n for some even n. She can try to build up Q. Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > őĽ n for some odd n.

Thus, we need to analyze the maximum possible Q given the linear budget, and vice versa.

We can think of the game as a "two-player" control problem. Since both players can choose 0, they can always avoid causing the opponent's loss. So if a player has a winning strategy, it must involve making a move that forces the opponent to be unable to move, and the opponent cannot avoid that because any move they make leads to a losing position. We need to analyze the game as a "control" problem.

Let's think of it as a "game of inequalities" where the players can "spend" their budget. The total "budget" for each player is not fixed but grows linearly. Actually, the constraints are that after each turn, the sum of the chosen numbers (linear) is at most őĽ n for odd n, and the sum of squares is at most n for even n. So we can think of the players as "sharing" the resource of the linear sum and the square sum. The game ends when either the linear sum exceeds the linear bound for an odd turn, or the square sum exceeds the square bound for an even turn.

Because the game is infinite, the players can play "safe" moves that keep both sums within bounds. The question is: Can one player force a violation of the opponent's bound? This is reminiscent of a "game of reaching a target" with two resources.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Alice to win for any őĽ > 1? But we already have őĽ=1.1, she can win quickly. For őĽ=0.9, we need to see if she can still win. But if Alice's strategy is to play 0 on all odd turns, then she never wins. So she must play positive numbers at some point. Thus, the critical őĽ is between 0.703 and 0.704. So őĽ0 ‚Čą 0.7035? Let's try to find the exact threshold. The pattern suggests that the threshold is őĽ = 1/‚ąö2? Actually, 1/‚ąö2 ‚Čą0.7071, but our simulations show that at őĽ=0.705 she wins, at őĽ=0.704 she wins, at őĽ=0.703 she loses. So threshold is around 0.703? That is less than 0.7071. Possibly the threshold is őĽ = (‚ąö2)/2? That is 0.7071, but we already saw őĽ=0.702 loses. So it's not that. Maybe it's őĽ = 1/‚ąö2? No, that's same.

Let's compute more precisely. Perhaps the critical őĽ is the solution to some equation. We need to find the exact value of őĽ for which the game is a draw (i.e., both can avoid losing indefinitely).
